Hong Kong: Agency (1920-1930)

Christian or Pro-Christian Leaders

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

                1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

                  Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

                  Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

                  ‚Äî

                  1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

                    Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

                    During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

                      Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

                      Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

                      In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

                      ‚Äî

                      1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

                        Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

                        During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

                        Ë´ãÂú®‰∏ãÊñπ „ÄåÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠î„Äç ÈÉ®ÂàÜÊèèËø∞Âú®ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÈáçË¶ÅÁöÑÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊàñË¶™Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÁµÑÁπîÊ©üÊßã„ÄÇ ÈÄô‰∫õÊ©üÊßãÂåÖÊã¨ÊúÄÊúâÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõÁöÑÊïôÊúÉ„ÄÅÊïôÊ¥æÁµÑÁπî„ÄÅÂÇ≥ÊïôÊ©üÊßã„ÄÅËÅØÁõü„ÄÅÂúòÈ´îÁ≠â„ÄÇ

                          2. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†ÈóúÊñºÊØè‰ΩçÁµÑÁπîÊ©üÊßãÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                        3. Êúâ‰∫õÁµÑÁπîÂèØËÉΩÊ¥ªË∫çÂú®Â§öÂÄã‰∏çÂêåÁöÑÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊôÇÊúü„ÄÇ Ë´ãÊ¶ÇËø∞ÂÖ∂Âú®ÂÖ∂‰ªñÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÂΩ±ÈüøÔºå‰ΩÜ‰∏ªË¶ÅÈóúÊ≥®ÂÖ∂Âú®Êú¨ÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÂΩ±Èüø„ÄÇ ÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊôÇÊúüÁöÑË≥áË®äÊúÉÂú®Â∞çÊáâÂïèÂç∑‰∏≠Êé°ÈõÜ„ÄÇ ‚òê


                        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

                        This compilation provides an overview of ten Christian organizations and churches in Hong Kong during the early 20th century. Each entry highlights the historical background, key developments, and contributions of these institutions to Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian community.

                        ‚Äî

                        Hong Kong Christian Population

                        (Sources: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2004: 4; Hong Kong Government 1920a, 1920b, 1929a, 1929b)

                        According to the Hong Kong Blue Book‚Äôs Ecclesiastical Return statistics, the number of attendees at Chinese Christian church services grew from 3,151 in 1920 to 4,020 in 1929. During the same period, attendance at non-Chinese churches rose from 1,655 to 1,695, representing a growth rate of 27.6% for Chinese churches, compared to just 2.4% for non-Chinese churches.

                        However, the statistics in the Hong Kong Blue Book only covered mainstream denominations, including the Anglican Church, London Missionary Society, Congregational Church, Methodist Church, Basel Mission, and Wesleyan Missionary Society.

                        In 1929, Hong Kong had a total population of 1,075,690, including 18,150 non-Chinese and 1,057,440 Chinese. This means that Chinese Christian worshippers accounted for approximately 0.38% of the Chinese population, a decrease from 0.64% in 1919. This decline was mainly due to a 117% growth in the Chinese population over the decade, which outpaced the growth in the number of Chinese Christians.

                        ‚Äî

                        1. The Sixth District of the Kwangtung Synod of the Church of Christ in China, 1920

                          (Sources: ÂºµÁ•ùÈΩ° 1921: 182‚Äì184; ÊãõËßÄÊµ∑ 1925: 68‚Äì70; Âê≥Áæ©ÈõÑ 2006; Merwin 1974: 70‚Äì72)

                          During the 1910s, the Presbyterian Church, London Missionary Society, and Congregational Church discussed forming a union. In July 1919, representatives from these denominations and others reached an agreement in Guangzhou, establishing the Kwangtung Synod of the Church of Christ in China. The Sixth District, covering Hong Kong, was established in 1920.

                          While the synod aimed to unify church governance, missionary societies continued to wield significant influence. By 1926, the synod was reorganized as the Guangdong Association, with Hong Kong remaining part of the Sixth District. After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, the Hong Kong District became independent of the Guangdong Association. By the 1970s, the Hong Kong District achieved financial independence and self-governance.

                          ‚Äî

                          1. Hong Kong YWCA, 1920

                            (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 274‚Äì276; ÈªÉÊÖßË≥¢ 2021)

                            The Hong Kong YWCA was officially founded in March 1920, following earlier initiatives at several girls‚Äô schools. It established a hostel for women travelers in 1921, literacy classes for domestic workers and factory women in 1922, and maternal health programs in 1923.

                            In the 1930s, the organization expanded to include community schools, advocacy for women‚Äôs rights, and publishing The Hong Kong Women‚Äôs Voice. It played a key role in achieving gender equality, with milestones such as equal pay for civil servants by 1969 and the legal enactment of monogamy in 1970.

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Basel Evangelical Missionary Society, 1923

                              (Source: ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì57)

                              The Basel Mission established seven congregations in Hong Kong by the early 20th century. In 1923, these congregations formed the Hong Kong Basel Synod, promoting self-governance. The synod was later integrated into the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, achieving full independence in 1929.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Kowloon Union Church, 1924

                                (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 156; Kowloon Union Church n.d.)

                                Established in 1924 for non-Anglican English-speaking Christians, the Kowloon Union Church built its current premises in Jordan Road in 1931, funded by donations and government support.

                                ‚Äî

                                1. CCC Hop Yat Church, 1926

                                  (Sources: ÂäâÁ¥πÈ∫ü 2003: 207‚Äì217; ÁéãË™å‰ø° 1986: 64‚Äì75)

                                  Established as a cooperative effort between the London Missionary Society and the Presbyterian Church, CCC Hop Yat Church replaced the outdated To Tsai Church. Construction of the new church began in 1922 and was completed in 1926.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Bible Book and Tract Depot, Hong Kong, 1927

                                    (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 246‚Äì247; ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014)

                                    The Bible Book and Tract Depot, founded in 1927 with support from Shanghai and Guangzhou Bible Societies, promoted Bible distribution in South China. Financial struggles in the 1930s were alleviated by community contributions.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Guangdong Congregational Mission Society, 1929

                                      (Source: ÁéãÈúáÂª∑ 2018: 4)

                                      This society was formed to oversee the work of Congregational churches in Guangdong Province after the American mission withdrew from the region in 1922. It supported over 30 congregations through local fundraising efforts.

                                      ‚Äî

                                      1. Hephzibah Mission, 1920s

                                        (Source: ÂñúÊ®ÇÁ¶èÈü≥Â†Ç 2002: 58‚Äì74)

                                        Established by faith missionaries in the 1920s, the Hephzibah Mission offered Bible studies and worship services in Sham Shui Po. It transitioned to full local leadership by the 1930s.

                                        ‚Äî

                                        1. CCC Mongkok Church, 1928

                                          (Source: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 122‚Äì126)

                                          Originally a branch of the Congregational Church, CCC Mongkok Church was formally established in 1928 with the support of the London Missionary Society and other local leaders.

                                          1. True Jesus Church, 1929

                                            (Source: ÁúüËÄ∂Á©åÊïôÊúÉ 1947: C56‚ÄìC59)

                                            The True Jesus Church was founded in Kowloon in 1929 by Chiu Maria, who had joined the denomination in Singapore. The church grew rapidly, establishing prayer houses and congregations in the New Territories before World War II.

    ### (1) The Anti-Mui Tsai Campaign

    (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 283‚Äì285; È∫•Ê¢ÖÁîü 1933; Ê•äÂúãÂº∑ 2010; ÈªÉÊÖßË≥¢ 2021: 63‚Äì77; Èô≥Â≠∏ÁÑ∂„ÄÅÂê≥ÂÆ∂Ë±™ 2024: 178‚Äì200; Miners 1990: 117‚Äì129)

    One of the most impactful pre-war social movements initiated by Hong Kong churches was the Anti-Mui Tsai Campaign. Mui tsai referred to a system of indentured servitude involving young girls sold into domestic servitude or, in some cases, prostitution. Rooted in poverty, patriarchal traditions, and even abduction, the practice posed long-standing social and gender issues in Chinese society.

    Although Hong Kong was under British colonial rule, the government, wary of disrupting influential local Chinese leaders, allowed the practice to continue. Efforts to abolish it gained traction in the late 19th century through humanitarian concerns expressed by expatriates.

    The movement gained momentum in the 1920s when Mrs. C. B. L. Haslewood, wife of a British naval officer, raised public awareness through articles and advocacy in Britain, pressuring the Hong Kong government to act. On July 31, 1921, a public meeting at the Tai Ping Theatre, attended by 300 people, revealed sharp divisions: Chinese elites sought to maintain the status quo, while a group of Christians opposed the practice.

    In August 1921, Christian leaders, including Yang Siu-Chuen and Mai Mei-Sang, founded the Anti-Mui Tsai Society, condemning the practice as inhumane and a violation of human rights. They advocated for legislative reforms and conducted public campaigns in schools, churches, and trade unions. Key figures such as Wong Mau-Lam, Chiu Wai-Chung, and Cheung Chu-Ling were instrumental in this decade-long struggle.

    The movement achieved significant milestones, starting with the Domestic Servants Ordinance of 1923 and culminating in stricter anti-trafficking laws in 1938. The campaign‚Äôs legacy includes publications such as The Mui Tsai Problem in Hong Kong (1923) and A Brief History of the Anti-Mui Tsai Movement (1933), preserving detailed accounts of this historic struggle.

    ‚Äî

    ### (2) The Founding and Closure of The Ta Kwong Weekly, 1926

    (Sources: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014: 239‚Äì240)

    In 1925, Christian journalist Cheung Yik-King returned to Hong Kong to lead Ta Kwong Pao at the invitation of chairman Wong Kwok-Shuen. To strengthen its religious focus, he rebranded a popular column into Enlightening Radiance, featuring religious essays and sermons.

    Realizing the limitations of a daily newspaper format for in-depth religious discourse, Cheung launched a separate weekly publication, The Ta Kwong Weekly, on February 28, 1926, dedicated to Christian themes such as theology, poetry, essays, and fiction. In his inaugural editorial, Cheung expressed hope that the weekly would foster scholarly contributions from both local and international Christian communities.

    Cheung resigned in November 1926, citing concerns over political interference in Ta Kwong Pao. Despite efforts by Cheung Chu-Ling and others to retain him, his departure marked the end of his involvement with the publication.

    ‚Äî

    ### (3) Lu Xun‚Äôs Lectures at the YMCA, 1927

    (Source: Âë®ÊÉ†Êñå 2009)

    On February 18 and 19, 1927, renowned Chinese writer Lu Xun delivered two lectures at the Chinese YMCA in Hong Kong, titled ‚ÄúSilent China‚Äù and ‚ÄúThe Tune is Played Out.‚Äù Both were attended by over 600 people, with live Cantonese interpretation by Xu Guangping.

    In ‚ÄúSilent China,‚Äù Lu Xun criticized the use of archaic classical Chinese, advocating for modern vernacular expression to transform China into a nation with a vibrant voice. In ‚ÄúThe Tune is Played Out,‚Äù he challenged outdated literary traditions and urged the youth to embrace freedom and reject conformity.

    Despite the lectures‚Äô success, Lu Xun left Hong Kong with a poor impression. In his essay Reflections on Hong Kong, he recounted obstacles faced by organizers, such as censorship and restricted access to lecture tickets by opponents. His critique of ‚Äúnational essence‚Äù antagonized local elites, leaving a contentious legacy.

    ### (1) Christ Issue

    (Source: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014: 240‚Äì241)

    The Christ Issue, a special publication edited by Cheung Chu-Ling, the pastor of To Tsai Church, was released annually at Christmas starting in 1921. According to Lau Yuet-Sang, a total of over ten issues were published.

    In the preface to the second issue, Cheung Chu-Ling explained the purpose of the publication:

    > ‚ÄúThe original aim of Christ Issue is to introduce the person of Christ to the intellectuals of Chinese society‚Ä¶ However, in this era of blurred distinctions between right and wrong, it is not easy to lead people to believe in Christ, the ideal and supreme figure. Take, for example, the unfortunate rise of the Anti-Christian Alliance among China‚Äôs educated youth earlier this year‚Ä¶ Christianity, in its narrow sense, is about saving individuals, but in its broader sense, it is about saving the nation. The principles of Christianity‚Äîhumility, purity, universal love, peace, equality, freedom, sacrifice, service, gentleness, perseverance, mutual assistance, loyalty, dedication, optimism, faith, and hope for eternal life‚Äîare foundational for both personal moral cultivation and public virtue. These principles are the foundation of true reform and national strength. Through the collective transformation of individuals into genuine reformers, we can build a supreme society and a resilient nation. Thus, Christ Issue dedicates itself to introducing Christ to our countrymen despite the challenges.‚Äù

    The publication featured diverse content, including essays, educational articles, devotional reflections, sermons, poems, fiction, and accounts of Christmas celebrations. Contributors to the second issue included notable figures like Cheung Chu-Ling, Lau Yat-Sam, Leung Kwan-Muk, Lie Yuk-Kim, Liao Xin-Min, Chan Sau-Ming, Chan Sau-Yuen, Yip Kai-Fong, Chan Kam-Yung, Mai Mei-Sang, Kan Yau-Man, and Ma Yiu-Tung. Some of these writers, such as Leung Kwan-Muk, Yip Kai-Fong, Chan Kam-Yung, and Kan Yau-Man, were active in China‚Äôs Christian intellectual circles at the time.

    In A History of Hong Kong Christianity, Lau Yuet-Sang praised the Christ Issue for making a significant contribution to the Chinese Christian literary scene.

    (1) The CCP Hong Kong Branch‚Äôs Anti-Christian Efforts

    (1923‚Äì1924)

    (Sources: Central Archives, Guangdong Archives 1982)

    After its establishment in 1921, the Chinese Communist Party (CCP) began operating in Hong Kong around 1923, forming a provisional branch led by Liang Pengwan. In an October report, Liang described Hong Kong as being dominated by Christianity and Confucianism, noting that church-affiliated schools and Christian organizations were pervasive, including 42 schools for the poor and outreach activities in factories and businesses.

    Despite these observations, the CCP initially adopted a strategy of ‚Äúdelayed opposition‚Äù toward Christianity due to a lack of personnel. Nonetheless, in November 1923, the Hong Kong branch decided to print anti-Christian pamphlets during Christmas, a move endorsed by the Guangdong Regional Committee. That Christmas, pamphlets titled The Crimes of Christianity were distributed, accusing Christianity of impeding science and progress, promoting imperialism, and oppressing workers.

    In early 1924, the CCP proposed forming student organizations to counter Christianity, such as the ‚ÄúStudent Friends Society.‚Äù Reports described confrontations between CCP activists and Christians, with heated debates becoming a common occurrence. A May report highlighted efforts to oppose Christian organizations, particularly the YMCA, while aiming to infiltrate church schools.

    Although these efforts mirrored the broader anti-Christian movement in China, they failed to mobilize large-scale support in Hong Kong, where Christian organizations maintained strong influence.

    ‚Äî

    ### (2) St. Paul‚Äôs College Strike Incident (1925)

    (Sources: Central Archives, Guangdong Archives 1982; Jiang Guansheng 2011: 49)

    In May 1925, the CCP Hong Kong branch reported on a failed student strike at St. Paul‚Äôs College. Many leading students withdrew or transferred to other schools following the strike. The CCP acknowledged that none of its members were enrolled at the college at the time, though they had begun activities within the school.

    The CCP Central Committee advised caution in engaging with English schools, urging discreet efforts to discourage students from aligning with imperialist values. After the May Thirtieth Incident in Shanghai, the Hong Kong branch intensified its focus on student movements, facilitating the formation of the ‚ÄúHong Kong Students‚Äô Federation.‚Äù While students from Queen‚Äôs College were the first to strike, St. Paul‚Äôs College students also participated by returning to their hometowns in support of the movement.

    These efforts reflected the CCP‚Äôs strategy of leveraging student movements to challenge imperialist education and religious influence, although their success in Hong Kong was limited.

Christian or Pro-Christian Events/Movements

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

Key Theological Issues

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

Anti-Christian Leaders

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

Anti-Christian Organizations

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

          ÂçìÊÅ©È´òÔºà1887‚Äì1985ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±Êñ∞ÂÆâ‰∫∫ÔºåÂπºÂπ¥Èö®Áà∂Ë¶™‰æÜÊ∏ØÔºåÂ∞±ËÆÄÊñºÊãîËêÉÊõ∏Èô¢„ÄÇ1903Âπ¥Ëµ∑Âú®ÂæãÂ∏´Ê®ì‰ªªÂÇ≥Ë≠ØÔºåËá≥ÈÄÄ‰ºë„ÄÇÂú®Â∑¥Ëâ≤Á∏ΩÊúÉÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊõæ‰ªªÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂè∏Â∫´Ôºà1924‚Äì26ÔºâÂèäÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç‰∏ªÂ∏≠Ôºà1926‚Äì1934Ôºâ„ÄÇ1928Âπ¥Â∑¥Ëâ≤Á∏ΩÊúÉÊòìÂêçÂ¥áÁúüÊúÉÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉÂæåÔºåÊ≠∑‰ªªÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉÊúÉÈï∑Ôºà1929‚Äì1934Ôºå1942‚Äì1947ÔºâÂèäÂâØÊúÉÈï∑Ôºà1948‚Äì1949Ôºå1952‚Äì1965Ôºâ„ÄÇÂ∞±‰ªªÊúÉÈï∑ÂæåÔºåÊé®ÂãïÊñ∞ÁïåÈñãËçíÔºåÂèäÂçîÂä©Â¥áË¨ôÂ†Ç„ÄÅÂúüÁìúÁÅ£Â†ÇÂèäÁ≠≤ÁÆïÁÅ£Â†ÇËá™Á´ãËá™È§ä„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊ∑™Èô∑ÊúüÈñìÔºåÊõæË¢´Êâ£ÊäºÊñºÈõÜ‰∏≠Ááü52Â§©„ÄÇ1947Âπ¥ÔºåÊì¨ÂÆöÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉ‰∫îÂπ¥Ë®àÂäÉÔºå‰∏¶ÁµÑÁπîÂÇ≥ÈÅìÈÉ®Ôºå‰ªªÂçÄÂÇ≥ÈÅìÈï∑„ÄÇÊúüÈñìÊúâË•øË≤¢Â†Ç„ÄÅÂçóËèØËéÜÂ†ÇÂèäË™øÊôØÂ∂∫Â†ÇÊàêÁ´ã„ÄÇ1948Âπ¥ÔºåÂèàË≥ºË≤∑Á¶èÈü≥ËªäÂèäÁ¶èÈü≥ËàπÔºå‰ΩúÊñ∞ÁïåÂèäÊµ∑‰∏ä‰ΩàÈÅì„ÄÇÊ≠§Â§ñÔºåÂèàÂèÉËàáÂâµËæ¶„ÄåÊ∏Ø‰πùÂüπÈùàÁ†îÁ∂ìÊúÉ„ÄçÔºåÊ≠∑‰ªªÂè∏Â∫´„ÄÇ

Anti-Christian Events/Movements

  1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

    Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

    Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

    ‚Äî

    1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

      Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

      ‚Äî

      1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

        Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

        Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

        In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

        ‚Äî

        1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

          Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

          During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

                1. Charles R. Duppuy (Èçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã 1951: 23-25; Yung 2021)

                  Charles R. Duppuy (1881‚Äì1944) served as the sixth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1920‚Äì1932). A graduate of Oxford University, he was ordained as a priest in 1904. In 1912, he became Secretary of the Society for the Propagation of the Gospel (SPG), and from 1914 to 1918, he served as a chaplain with the British Army in France. In June 1920, he was consecrated as bishop at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral, London, and arrived in Hong Kong in November. To promote understanding and support for the Hong Kong Anglican Church among English parishioners, he established the Victoria Diocesan Association and published the Outpost.

                  Bishop Duppuy advocated for the self-governance of the church, aiming for the unification of the separate English and Chinese diocesan councils and proposing the consecration of Chinese bishops at an appropriate time. He also supported the ordination of female deaconesses and, in December 1931, ordained J. L. Vincent as the first female deaconess of the diocese, while also championing the participation of female deaconesses in diocesan meetings.

                  ‚Äî

                  1. Harry Lechmere Clift (Biographical Dictionary n.d.)

                    Harry Lechmere Clift (1876‚Äì1949) was a missionary of the Emmanuel Medical Mission. Dr. Clift and his wife came to Guangxi, China, in 1906 to carry out missionary work in Nanning. In 1923, they moved to Hong Kong, where Dr. Clift became the Visiting Medical Officer to Chinese Hospitals and Dispensaries. In 1929, he established the Emmanuel English Bookroom and a medical clinic on Nathan Road, which hosted English Bible study groups and worship services.

                    During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, Dr. Clift was interned at Stanley Internment Camp. He passed away in Hong Kong in 1949 at the age of 73.

                    ‚Äî

                    1. Cheung Sing Wo (ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 340‚Äì344; ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì53; ÈÑ∫Êô∫ËâØ 2018; ÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç 1967: 83‚Äì84; ÂäâÂøóÂÅâ 1999: 156)

                      Cheung Sing Wo (1853‚Äì1938), a pastor from Dongguan, Guangdong, was the son of Cheung Choi Ting, a Christian convert baptized by August Hanspach. Cheung Choi Ting had served as a senior official during the Taiping Heavenly Kingdom before his martyrdom in Hangzhou.

                      Cheung Sing Wo studied theology at the Basel Mission Seminary in Lilang and graduated at 23. He began his ministry with Rudolf Leckler and later served at various churches in Guangdong and Hong Kong, including overseeing congregations in Shau Kei Wan and Sai Ying Pun. In 1895, he was ordained and returned to serve the Sai Ying Pun congregation for 14 years, while also assisting other churches in Hong Kong.

                      In 1923, he was elected the first president of the Hong Kong Basel Mission Synod and participated in drafting its constitution. He retired in 1921 and returned to his hometown, where he passed away in 1938. His tombstone bears the inscription, ‚ÄúDedicating himself to the church, even unto death.‚Äù

                      ‚Äî

                      1. Cheuk Yan Ko (ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 57‚Äì60)

                        Cheuk Yan Ko (1887‚Äì1985), a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong with his father as a child and studied at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. He worked as an interpreter at a law firm from 1903 until his retirement. Cheuk served as Treasurer of the Basel Mission Synod (1924‚Äì1926) and Chairman of the Kau Yan Church (1926‚Äì1934). After the Basel Synod was renamed the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, he held various leadership roles, including President of the Hong Kong District (1929‚Äì1934, 1942‚Äì1947) and Vice President (1948‚Äì1949, 1952‚Äì1965).

                        During his presidency, he promoted self-sufficiency among churches and oversaw the establishment of new congregations in areas such as Shau Kei Wan, To Kwa Wan, and the New Territories. During the Japanese occupation, he was detained for 52 days in a concentration camp. In 1947, he devised a five-year plan for the Hong Kong District, organized the Evangelistic Department, and oversaw the founding of congregations in Sai Kung, Nam Wah Po, and Tiu Keng Leng. Additionally, he introduced a mobile gospel car and ship for outreach in the New Territories and maritime areas. Cheuk also co-founded the Hong Kong and Kowloon Keswick Convention and served as its Treasurer.

                        ÂºµËÅ≤ÂíåÔºà1853-1938ÔºâÁâßÂ∏´ÔºåÂª£Êù±Êù±Ëéû‰∫∫„ÄÇÂÖ∂Áà∂ÂºµÂΩ©Âª∑ÁÇ∫Âü∫Áù£ÂæíÔºåÂèóÊ¥óÊñºÈüìÂ£´‰ºØÔºàAugust HanspachÔºâÔºåÊõæÂèÉÂä†Â§™Âπ≥Â§©ÂúãÔºåÊéàÊà∂ÈÉ®‰æçÈÉéÔºåÂæåÊñºÊù≠Â∑ûÊÆâÈõ£„ÄÇÂºµËÅ≤ÂíåÂπ¥ÈùíÊôÇÊñºÊùéÊúóÂ∑¥Ëâ≤ÊúÉÂ≠òÁúüÊõ∏Èô¢ÔºàÂæåÊó©ÂêçÂÇ≥ÈÅìÊõ∏Èô¢ÔºâÊé•ÂèóÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÊïôËÇ≤ÔºåÂªø‰∏âÊ≠≤Ôºà1876ÔºâÊôÇÁï¢Ê•≠ÂæåÔºåÈö®ÈªéÂäõÂü∫ÔºàRodolf LecklerÔºâ‰ªªÂÇ≥ÈÅì„ÄÇÂæåÂèóËÅ∑ÊñºÈï∑Â±±Âè£Â†ÇÔºåÂÖºÁêÜÈæçÊ±ü‰ªî„ÄÅÁâõÁú†ÂüîÂÖ©Âü∫ÂùÄ„ÄÇ‰∫îÂπ¥ÂæåÔºåÈ¶ñÊ¨°Ë™øËá≥È¶ôÊ∏ØÔºåÊé•ÊõøÈÄÄ‰ºëÁöÑÊùéÊ≠£È´òÁâßÂ∏´ÔºåÂÖºÁêÜÁ≠≤ÁÆïÁÅ£Â†ÇÊïôÂãô„ÄÇ1883Âπ¥ÂÜçË™øÂà∞Êù±Ê±üÊ∞∏ÂÆâÁ∏£Âè§Á´π„ÄÅÁü≥Âªà‰∏ÄÂ∏∂ÂÇ≥Êïô„ÄÇ1895Âπ¥ÊåâÁâßÔºåÂÜçÊ¨°Ë™øËá≥È¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∏ΩÊúÉÔºåÊñºË•øÁáüÁõ§Â†ÇÊúç‰∫ãÔºåÈÅîÂçÅÂõõÂπ¥‰πã‰πÖ„ÄÇÂêåÊôÇÔºå‰ªñÂèàÂçîÂä©Á≠≤ÁÆïÁÅ£„ÄÅÊ∑±Ê∞¥ÂüóÂèäÂúüÁìúÁÅ£ÊïôÂãô„ÄÇ

                        (4) ÂçìÊÅ©È´òÔºàÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©©2012Ôºö57-60Ôºâ

                        ÂçìÊÅ©È´òÔºà1887‚Äì1985ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±Êñ∞ÂÆâ‰∫∫ÔºåÂπºÂπ¥Èö®Áà∂Ë¶™‰æÜÊ∏ØÔºåÂ∞±ËÆÄÊñºÊãîËêÉÊõ∏Èô¢„ÄÇ1903Âπ¥Ëµ∑Âú®ÂæãÂ∏´Ê®ì‰ªªÂÇ≥Ë≠ØÔºåËá≥ÈÄÄ‰ºë„ÄÇÂú®Â∑¥Ëâ≤Á∏ΩÊúÉÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊõæ‰ªªÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂè∏Â∫´Ôºà1924‚Äì26ÔºâÂèäÊïëÊÅ©Â†Ç‰∏ªÂ∏≠Ôºà1926‚Äì1934Ôºâ„ÄÇ1928Âπ¥Â∑¥Ëâ≤Á∏ΩÊúÉÊòìÂêçÂ¥áÁúüÊúÉÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉÂæåÔºåÊ≠∑‰ªªÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉÊúÉÈï∑Ôºà1929‚Äì1934Ôºå1942‚Äì1947ÔºâÂèäÂâØÊúÉÈï∑Ôºà1948‚Äì1949Ôºå1952‚Äì1965Ôºâ„ÄÇÂ∞±‰ªªÊúÉÈï∑ÂæåÔºåÊé®ÂãïÊñ∞ÁïåÈñãËçíÔºåÂèäÂçîÂä©Â¥áË¨ôÂ†Ç„ÄÅÂúüÁìúÁÅ£Â†ÇÂèäÁ≠≤ÁÆïÁÅ£Â†ÇËá™Á´ãËá™È§ä„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊ∑™Èô∑ÊúüÈñìÔºåÊõæË¢´Êâ£ÊäºÊñºÈõÜ‰∏≠Ááü52Â§©„ÄÇ1947Âπ¥ÔºåÊì¨ÂÆöÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂçÄÊúÉ‰∫îÂπ¥Ë®àÂäÉÔºå‰∏¶ÁµÑÁπîÂÇ≥ÈÅìÈÉ®Ôºå‰ªªÂçÄÂÇ≥ÈÅìÈï∑„ÄÇÊúüÈñìÊúâË•øË≤¢Â†Ç„ÄÅÂçóËèØËéÜÂ†ÇÂèäË™øÊôØÂ∂∫Â†ÇÊàêÁ´ã„ÄÇ1948Âπ¥ÔºåÂèàË≥ºË≤∑Á¶èÈü≥ËªäÂèäÁ¶èÈü≥ËàπÔºå‰ΩúÊñ∞ÁïåÂèäÊµ∑‰∏ä‰ΩàÈÅì„ÄÇÊ≠§Â§ñÔºåÂèàÂèÉËàáÂâµËæ¶„ÄåÊ∏Ø‰πùÂüπÈùàÁ†îÁ∂ìÊúÉ„ÄçÔºåÊ≠∑‰ªªÂè∏Â∫´„ÄÇ

                        Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÁµÑÁπîÔºàÂåÖÊã¨Ë¶™Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÁµÑÁπîÔºâ

                        Ë´ãÂú®‰∏ãÊñπ „ÄåÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠î„Äç ÈÉ®ÂàÜÊèèËø∞Âú®ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÈáçË¶ÅÁöÑÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊàñË¶™Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÁµÑÁπîÊ©üÊßã„ÄÇ ÈÄô‰∫õÊ©üÊßãÂåÖÊã¨ÊúÄÊúâÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõÁöÑÊïôÊúÉ„ÄÅÊïôÊ¥æÁµÑÁπî„ÄÅÂÇ≥ÊïôÊ©üÊßã„ÄÅËÅØÁõü„ÄÅÂúòÈ´îÁ≠â„ÄÇ

                        ÔºàË´ãÂú®ÂÆåÊàêÁõ∏ÊáâÁöÑË¶ÅÊ±ÇÂêéÊ®ôË®òÊñáÂêéË§áÈÅ∏Ê°ÜÔºâÔºö

                        Ë´ãÊ†πÊìö‰ª•‰∏ãË¶ÅÊ±ÇÁµÑÁπîÊÇ®ÁöÑÁ≠îÊ°àÔºå‰∏¶ÂãæÈÅ∏Â∞çÊáâË§áÈÅ∏Ê°Ü.

                        1. Ë´ãÂàóÂá∫ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìËá≥Â∞ë‰∏âÂÄãÔºåËá≥Â§öÂçÅÂÄãÁµÑÁπî„ÄÇ ‚òê

                          1. Ë´ãÂåÖÊã¨ÈÄô‰∫õÁµÑÁπîÁöÑ‰∏≠ÊñáÂêçÁ®±ÂíåËã±ÊñáÊãºÂØ´ÔºàÂ∑≤Áü•ÊÖ£Áî®ÊãºÂØ´Ôºâ

                        2. Ë´ãÁ∏ΩÁµêÊ¶ÇËø∞ÊØèÂÄãÁµÑÁπîÂú®ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÁöÑÈóúÈçµË≤¢Áçª/ÂΩ±Èüø ÔºàÊØèÂÄãÁµÑÁπî500-1,000 Â≠óÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                          1. Ë´ãÁõ°ÂèØËÉΩÂåÖÊã¨ÂÆöÈáèË≥áË®äÂíåÊï∏ÊìöÔºåÊØîÂ¶ÇÁõ∏ÈóúÊïôÊúÉÂíåÂÄã‰∫∫ÁöÑÊï∏ÁõÆÁ≠â„ÄÇ ‚òê

                          2. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†ÈóúÊñºÊØè‰ΩçÁµÑÁπîÊ©üÊßãÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                        3. Êúâ‰∫õÁµÑÁπîÂèØËÉΩÊ¥ªË∫çÂú®Â§öÂÄã‰∏çÂêåÁöÑÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊôÇÊúü„ÄÇ Ë´ãÊ¶ÇËø∞ÂÖ∂Âú®ÂÖ∂‰ªñÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÂΩ±ÈüøÔºå‰ΩÜ‰∏ªË¶ÅÈóúÊ≥®ÂÖ∂Âú®Êú¨ÂçÅÂπ¥ÁöÑÂΩ±Èüø„ÄÇ ÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠∑Âè≤ÊôÇÊúüÁöÑË≥áË®äÊúÉÂú®Â∞çÊáâÂïèÂç∑‰∏≠Êé°ÈõÜ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                        This compilation provides an overview of ten Christian organizations and churches in Hong Kong during the early 20th century. Each entry highlights the historical background, key developments, and contributions of these institutions to Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian community.

                        ‚Äî

                        Hong Kong Christian Population

                        (Sources: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2004: 4; Hong Kong Government 1920a, 1920b, 1929a, 1929b)

                        According to the Hong Kong Blue Book‚Äôs Ecclesiastical Return statistics, the number of attendees at Chinese Christian church services grew from 3,151 in 1920 to 4,020 in 1929. During the same period, attendance at non-Chinese churches rose from 1,655 to 1,695, representing a growth rate of 27.6% for Chinese churches, compared to just 2.4% for non-Chinese churches.

                        However, the statistics in the Hong Kong Blue Book only covered mainstream denominations, including the Anglican Church, London Missionary Society, Congregational Church, Methodist Church, Basel Mission, and Wesleyan Missionary Society.

                        In 1929, Hong Kong had a total population of 1,075,690, including 18,150 non-Chinese and 1,057,440 Chinese. This means that Chinese Christian worshippers accounted for approximately 0.38% of the Chinese population, a decrease from 0.64% in 1919. This decline was mainly due to a 117% growth in the Chinese population over the decade, which outpaced the growth in the number of Chinese Christians.

                        ‚Äî

                        1. The Sixth District of the Kwangtung Synod of the Church of Christ in China, 1920

                          (Sources: ÂºµÁ•ùÈΩ° 1921: 182‚Äì184; ÊãõËßÄÊµ∑ 1925: 68‚Äì70; Âê≥Áæ©ÈõÑ 2006; Merwin 1974: 70‚Äì72)

                          During the 1910s, the Presbyterian Church, London Missionary Society, and Congregational Church discussed forming a union. In July 1919, representatives from these denominations and others reached an agreement in Guangzhou, establishing the Kwangtung Synod of the Church of Christ in China. The Sixth District, covering Hong Kong, was established in 1920.

                          While the synod aimed to unify church governance, missionary societies continued to wield significant influence. By 1926, the synod was reorganized as the Guangdong Association, with Hong Kong remaining part of the Sixth District. After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, the Hong Kong District became independent of the Guangdong Association. By the 1970s, the Hong Kong District achieved financial independence and self-governance.

                          ‚Äî

                          1. Hong Kong YWCA, 1920

                            (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 274‚Äì276; ÈªÉÊÖßË≥¢ 2021)

                            The Hong Kong YWCA was officially founded in March 1920, following earlier initiatives at several girls‚Äô schools. It established a hostel for women travelers in 1921, literacy classes for domestic workers and factory women in 1922, and maternal health programs in 1923.

                            In the 1930s, the organization expanded to include community schools, advocacy for women‚Äôs rights, and publishing The Hong Kong Women‚Äôs Voice. It played a key role in achieving gender equality, with milestones such as equal pay for civil servants by 1969 and the legal enactment of monogamy in 1970.

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Basel Evangelical Missionary Society, 1923

                              (Source: ÊπØÊ≥≥Ë©© 2012: 51‚Äì57)

                              The Basel Mission established seven congregations in Hong Kong by the early 20th century. In 1923, these congregations formed the Hong Kong Basel Synod, promoting self-governance. The synod was later integrated into the Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong, achieving full independence in 1929.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Kowloon Union Church, 1924

                                (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 156; Kowloon Union Church n.d.)

                                Established in 1924 for non-Anglican English-speaking Christians, the Kowloon Union Church built its current premises in Jordan Road in 1931, funded by donations and government support.

                                ‚Äî

                                1. CCC Hop Yat Church, 1926

                                  (Sources: ÂäâÁ¥πÈ∫ü 2003: 207‚Äì217; ÁéãË™å‰ø° 1986: 64‚Äì75)

                                  Established as a cooperative effort between the London Missionary Society and the Presbyterian Church, CCC Hop Yat Church replaced the outdated To Tsai Church. Construction of the new church began in 1922 and was completed in 1926.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Bible Book and Tract Depot, Hong Kong, 1927

                                    (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 246‚Äì247; ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014)

                                    The Bible Book and Tract Depot, founded in 1927 with support from Shanghai and Guangzhou Bible Societies, promoted Bible distribution in South China. Financial struggles in the 1930s were alleviated by community contributions.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Guangdong Congregational Mission Society, 1929

                                      (Source: ÁéãÈúáÂª∑ 2018: 4)

                                      This society was formed to oversee the work of Congregational churches in Guangdong Province after the American mission withdrew from the region in 1922. It supported over 30 congregations through local fundraising efforts.

                                      ‚Äî

                                      1. Hephzibah Mission, 1920s

                                        (Source: ÂñúÊ®ÇÁ¶èÈü≥Â†Ç 2002: 58‚Äì74)

                                        Established by faith missionaries in the 1920s, the Hephzibah Mission offered Bible studies and worship services in Sham Shui Po. It transitioned to full local leadership by the 1930s.

                                        ‚Äî

                                        1. CCC Mongkok Church, 1928

                                          (Source: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 122‚Äì126)

                                          Originally a branch of the Congregational Church, CCC Mongkok Church was formally established in 1928 with the support of the London Missionary Society and other local leaders.

                                          ‚Äî

                                          1. True Jesus Church, 1929

                                            (Source: ÁúüËÄ∂Á©åÊïôÊúÉ 1947: C56‚ÄìC59)

                                            The True Jesus Church was founded in Kowloon in 1929 by Chiu Maria, who had joined the denomination in Singapore. The church grew rapidly, establishing prayer houses and congregations in the New Territories before World War II.

    ### (1) The Anti-Mui Tsai Campaign

    (Sources: ÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤ 1996: 283‚Äì285; È∫•Ê¢ÖÁîü 1933; Ê•äÂúãÂº∑ 2010; ÈªÉÊÖßË≥¢ 2021: 63‚Äì77; Èô≥Â≠∏ÁÑ∂„ÄÅÂê≥ÂÆ∂Ë±™ 2024: 178‚Äì200; Miners 1990: 117‚Äì129)

    One of the most impactful pre-war social movements initiated by Hong Kong churches was the Anti-Mui Tsai Campaign. Mui tsai referred to a system of indentured servitude involving young girls sold into domestic servitude or, in some cases, prostitution. Rooted in poverty, patriarchal traditions, and even abduction, the practice posed long-standing social and gender issues in Chinese society.

    Although Hong Kong was under British colonial rule, the government, wary of disrupting influential local Chinese leaders, allowed the practice to continue. Efforts to abolish it gained traction in the late 19th century through humanitarian concerns expressed by expatriates.

    The movement gained momentum in the 1920s when Mrs. C. B. L. Haslewood, wife of a British naval officer, raised public awareness through articles and advocacy in Britain, pressuring the Hong Kong government to act. On July 31, 1921, a public meeting at the Tai Ping Theatre, attended by 300 people, revealed sharp divisions: Chinese elites sought to maintain the status quo, while a group of Christians opposed the practice.

    In August 1921, Christian leaders, including Yang Siu-Chuen and Mai Mei-Sang, founded the Anti-Mui Tsai Society, condemning the practice as inhumane and a violation of human rights. They advocated for legislative reforms and conducted public campaigns in schools, churches, and trade unions. Key figures such as Wong Mau-Lam, Chiu Wai-Chung, and Cheung Chu-Ling were instrumental in this decade-long struggle.

    The movement achieved significant milestones, starting with the Domestic Servants Ordinance of 1923 and culminating in stricter anti-trafficking laws in 1938. The campaign‚Äôs legacy includes publications such as The Mui Tsai Problem in Hong Kong (1923) and A Brief History of the Anti-Mui Tsai Movement (1933), preserving detailed accounts of this historic struggle.

    ‚Äî

    ### (2) The Founding and Closure of The Ta Kwong Weekly, 1926

    (Sources: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014: 239‚Äì240)

    In 1925, Christian journalist Cheung Yik-King returned to Hong Kong to lead Ta Kwong Pao at the invitation of chairman Wong Kwok-Shuen. To strengthen its religious focus, he rebranded a popular column into Enlightening Radiance, featuring religious essays and sermons.

    Realizing the limitations of a daily newspaper format for in-depth religious discourse, Cheung launched a separate weekly publication, The Ta Kwong Weekly, on February 28, 1926, dedicated to Christian themes such as theology, poetry, essays, and fiction. In his inaugural editorial, Cheung expressed hope that the weekly would foster scholarly contributions from both local and international Christian communities.

    Cheung resigned in November 1926, citing concerns over political interference in Ta Kwong Pao. Despite efforts by Cheung Chu-Ling and others to retain him, his departure marked the end of his involvement with the publication.

    ‚Äî

    ### (3) Lu Xun‚Äôs Lectures at the YMCA, 1927

    (Source: Âë®ÊÉ†Êñå 2009)

    On February 18 and 19, 1927, renowned Chinese writer Lu Xun delivered two lectures at the Chinese YMCA in Hong Kong, titled ‚ÄúSilent China‚Äù and ‚ÄúThe Tune is Played Out.‚Äù Both were attended by over 600 people, with live Cantonese interpretation by Xu Guangping.

    In ‚ÄúSilent China,‚Äù Lu Xun criticized the use of archaic classical Chinese, advocating for modern vernacular expression to transform China into a nation with a vibrant voice. In ‚ÄúThe Tune is Played Out,‚Äù he challenged outdated literary traditions and urged the youth to embrace freedom and reject conformity.

    Despite the lectures‚Äô success, Lu Xun left Hong Kong with a poor impression. In his essay Reflections on Hong Kong, he recounted obstacles faced by organizers, such as censorship and restricted access to lecture tickets by opponents. His critique of ‚Äúnational essence‚Äù antagonized local elites, leaving a contentious legacy.

    ### (1) Christ Issue

    (Source: ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2014: 240‚Äì241)

    The Christ Issue, a special publication edited by Cheung Chu-Ling, the pastor of To Tsai Church, was released annually at Christmas starting in 1921. According to Lau Yuet-Sang, a total of over ten issues were published.

    In the preface to the second issue, Cheung Chu-Ling explained the purpose of the publication:

    > ‚ÄúThe original aim of Christ Issue is to introduce the person of Christ to the intellectuals of Chinese society‚Ä¶ However, in this era of blurred distinctions between right and wrong, it is not easy to lead people to believe in Christ, the ideal and supreme figure. Take, for example, the unfortunate rise of the Anti-Christian Alliance among China‚Äôs educated youth earlier this year‚Ä¶ Christianity, in its narrow sense, is about saving individuals, but in its broader sense, it is about saving the nation. The principles of Christianity‚Äîhumility, purity, universal love, peace, equality, freedom, sacrifice, service, gentleness, perseverance, mutual assistance, loyalty, dedication, optimism, faith, and hope for eternal life‚Äîare foundational for both personal moral cultivation and public virtue. These principles are the foundation of true reform and national strength. Through the collective transformation of individuals into genuine reformers, we can build a supreme society and a resilient nation. Thus, Christ Issue dedicates itself to introducing Christ to our countrymen despite the challenges.‚Äù

    The publication featured diverse content, including essays, educational articles, devotional reflections, sermons, poems, fiction, and accounts of Christmas celebrations. Contributors to the second issue included notable figures like Cheung Chu-Ling, Lau Yat-Sam, Leung Kwan-Muk, Lie Yuk-Kim, Liao Xin-Min, Chan Sau-Ming, Chan Sau-Yuen, Yip Kai-Fong, Chan Kam-Yung, Mai Mei-Sang, Kan Yau-Man, and Ma Yiu-Tung. Some of these writers, such as Leung Kwan-Muk, Yip Kai-Fong, Chan Kam-Yung, and Kan Yau-Man, were active in China‚Äôs Christian intellectual circles at the time.

    In A History of Hong Kong Christianity, Lau Yuet-Sang praised the Christ Issue for making a significant contribution to the Chinese Christian literary scene.

    (1) The CCP Hong Kong Branch‚Äôs Anti-Christian Efforts

    (1923‚Äì1924)

    (Sources: Central Archives, Guangdong Archives 1982)

    After its establishment in 1921, the Chinese Communist Party (CCP) began operating in Hong Kong around 1923, forming a provisional branch led by Liang Pengwan. In an October report, Liang described Hong Kong as being dominated by Christianity and Confucianism, noting that church-affiliated schools and Christian organizations were pervasive, including 42 schools for the poor and outreach activities in factories and businesses.

    Despite these observations, the CCP initially adopted a strategy of ‚Äúdelayed opposition‚Äù toward Christianity due to a lack of personnel. Nonetheless, in November 1923, the Hong Kong branch decided to print anti-Christian pamphlets during Christmas, a move endorsed by the Guangdong Regional Committee. That Christmas, pamphlets titled The Crimes of Christianity were distributed, accusing Christianity of impeding science and progress, promoting imperialism, and oppressing workers.

    In early 1924, the CCP proposed forming student organizations to counter Christianity, such as the ‚ÄúStudent Friends Society.‚Äù Reports described confrontations between CCP activists and Christians, with heated debates becoming a common occurrence. A May report highlighted efforts to oppose Christian organizations, particularly the YMCA, while aiming to infiltrate church schools.

    Although these efforts mirrored the broader anti-Christian movement in China, they failed to mobilize large-scale support in Hong Kong, where Christian organizations maintained strong influence.

    ‚Äî

    ### (2) St. Paul‚Äôs College Strike Incident (1925)

    (Sources: Central Archives, Guangdong Archives 1982; Jiang Guansheng 2011: 49)

    In May 1925, the CCP Hong Kong branch reported on a failed student strike at St. Paul‚Äôs College. Many leading students withdrew or transferred to other schools following the strike. The CCP acknowledged that none of its members were enrolled at the college at the time, though they had begun activities within the school.

    The CCP Central Committee advised caution in engaging with English schools, urging discreet efforts to discourage students from aligning with imperialist values. After the May Thirtieth Incident in Shanghai, the Hong Kong branch intensified its focus on student movements, facilitating the formation of the ‚ÄúHong Kong Students‚Äô Federation.‚Äù While students from Queen‚Äôs College were the first to strike, St. Paul‚Äôs College students also participated by returning to their hometowns in support of the movement.

    These efforts reflected the CCP‚Äôs strategy of leveraging student movements to challenge imperialist education and religious influence, although their success in Hong Kong was limited.
